<p><b>Burundi</b> <span class="nowrap"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'b' in 'buy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">b</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ə</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'r' in 'rye'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">r</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ʊ/ short 'oo' in 'foot'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ʊ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">n</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'d' in 'dye'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">d</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ɨ/ 'e' in 'roses'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɨ</span></a></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'b' in 'buy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">b</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ə</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'r' in 'rye'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">r</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ʌ/ short 'u' in 'bud'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ʌ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">n</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'d' in 'dye'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">d</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">i</span></a></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span>, officially the <b>Republic of Burundi</b> (<a href="/wiki/Kirundi_language" title="Kirundi language" class="mw-redirect">Kirundi</a>: <span lang="rn"><i>Republika y'Uburundi</i></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[buˈɾundi]</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr"><i>République du Burundi</i></span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_French" title="Help:IPA for French">[buʁunˈdi]</a></span> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_French" title="Help:IPA for French">[byʁynˈdi]</a></span>), is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> in the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Africa" title="Southeast Africa" class="mw-redirect">Southeast Africa</a>, bordered by <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> to the east and south, and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> to the west. It is also sometimes considered part of <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a>. Burundi's capital is <a href="/wiki/Bujumbura" title="Bujumbura">Bujumbura</a>. Although the country is landlocked, much of the southwestern border is adjacent to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Twa" title="Great Lakes Twa">Twa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsi</a> peoples have lived in Burundi for at least five hundred years. For more than 200 years, Burundi had an indigenous <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burundi" title="Kingdom of Burundi">kingdom</a>. At the beginning of the twentieth century, <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a> colonized the region. After the First World War and its defeat, it ceded the territory to <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>. The latter ruled Burundi and Rwanda as a European colony known as <a href="/wiki/Ruanda-Urundi" title="Ruanda-Urundi">Ruanda-Urundi</a>. Their intervention exacerbated social differences between the Tutsi and Hutu, which contributed to political unrest in the region. There was civil war in Burundi as it fought for independence in the middle of the twentieth century. Presently, Burundi is governed as a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>.</p>
<p>Burundi is one of the five <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">poorest countries in the world</a>. It has one of the lowest <a href="/wiki/Per_capita_GDP" title="Per capita GDP" class="mw-redirect">per capita GDPs</a> of any nation in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-xlix_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xlix-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> The country has suffered from warfare, corruption and poor access to education. Burundi is densely populated and has had substantial <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a> as young people seek opportunities elsewhere. According to a 2012 <i>DHL Global Connectedness Index</i>, Burundi is the least globalized of 140 surveyed countries.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Global_Hunger_Index" title="Global Hunger Index">Global Hunger Index</a> of 2013, Burundi has an indicator ratio of 38.8, earning the nation the distinction of being the hungriest country in the world in terms of percentage.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Burundi" title="History of Burundi">History of Burundi</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Burundi">Kingdom of Burundi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Burundi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section requires <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit">expansion</a>. <small><i>(September 2012)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burundi" title="Kingdom of Burundi">Kingdom of Burundi</a></div>
<p>The last Burundian monarchy is said to have begun in the late 17th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonization">Colonization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Colonization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruanda-Urundi" title="Ruanda-Urundi">Ruanda-Urundi</a></div>
<p>Germany established armed forces in Ruanda and Burundi at the end of the 19th century, colonizing the area and establishing German East Africa. After being defeated in World War I, Germany was forced to cede "control" of a section of the former <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On October 20, 1924, this land, which consisted of modern-day Rwanda and Burundi, became a Belgian <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> mandate territory. In practical terms it was considered part of the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_colonial_empire" title="Belgian colonial empire">Belgian colonial empire</a>, and was known as <a href="/wiki/Ruanda-Urundi" title="Ruanda-Urundi">Ruanda-Urundi</a>. The Belgians allowed Ruanda-Urundi to continue its kingship dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wein5_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wein5-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Following World War II, Ruanda-Urundi was classified as a United Nations Trust Territory under Belgian administrative authority.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> During the 1940s, a series of policies caused divisions throughout the country. On October 4, 1943, powers were split in the legislative division of Burundi's government between chiefdoms and lower chiefdoms. Chiefdoms were in charge of land, and lower sub-chiefdoms were established. Native authorities also had powers.<sup id="cite_ref-wein5_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wein5-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1948, Belgium allowed the region to form political parties.<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> These factions contributed to gaining Burundi's independence from Belgium.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_and_civil_war">Independence and civil war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Independence and civil war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Burundian_Genocide" title="Burundian Genocide">Burundian Genocide</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Burundi_(1962-1966).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Flag_of_Burundi_%281962-1966%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Burundi_%281962-1966%29.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Flag_of_Burundi_%281962-1966%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Burundi_%281962-1966%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Flag_of_Burundi_%281962-1966%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Burundi_%281962-1966%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Burundi_(1962-1966).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Flag of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burundi" title="Kingdom of Burundi">Kingdom of Burundi</a> (1962–1966).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG/220px-Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG/330px-Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG/440px-Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Independence Square and monument in Bujumbura.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On January 20, 1959, Burundi's ruler <a href="/wiki/Mwambutsa_IV_Bangiriceng_of_Burundi" title="Mwambutsa IV Bangiriceng of Burundi">Mwami Mwambutsa IV</a> requested from the Belgian Minister of Colonies a separation of Burundi and Rwanda and a dissolution of Ruanda-Urundi.<sup id="cite_ref-wein7_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wein7-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Six months later, political parties were formed to bring attention to Burundi's independence from Europe and to separate Rwanda from Burundi.<sup id="cite_ref-wein7_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wein7-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> The first of these political parties was the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_National_Progress" title="Union for National Progress">Union for National Progress</a> (UPRONA).</p>
<p>Burundi's push for independence was influenced by the instability and ethnic persecution suffered by its peoples in Rwanda. In November 1959, Rwandese <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a> attacked the <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rwanda_Revolution" title="Rwanda Revolution" class="mw-redirect">massacred</a> them by the thousands. Many Tutsi escaped to Uganda and Burundi to find freedom from persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-people60_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-people60-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The Hutu took power in Rwanda by winning the Belgian-run elections in 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The UPRONA, a multi-ethnic unity party led by Prince <a href="/wiki/Louis_Rwagasore" title="Louis Rwagasore" class="mw-redirect">Louis Rwagasore</a> and the Christian Democratic Party, (PDC) became the most prominent organization throughout Burundi-Urundi. After UPRONA's victory in legislative elections, Prince Rwagasore was assassinated on October 13 in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The country claimed independence on July 1, 1962,<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> and legally changed its name from Ruanda-Urundi to Burundi.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> Mwami Mwambutsa IV was named king.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> On September 18, 1962, just over two months after declaring independence from Belgium, Burundi joined the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Upon Burundi’s independence, it established a constitutional monarchy. Both Hutu and Tutsi peoples were represented in parliament, but political instability has contributed to ethnic tensions. When King Mwambutsa appointed a Tutsi prime minister, the Hutu felt cheated, as they held the majority of seats in parliament. An ensuing attempted <i><a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a></i> by the Hutu-dominated police was ruthlessly suppressed by the Army, then led by Tutsi officer Captain <a href="/wiki/Michel_Micombero" title="Michel Micombero">Michel Micombero</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> When Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Ngendandumwe" title="Pierre Ngendandumwe">Pierre Ngendandumwe</a> (Hutu) was assassinated in 1965, Hutu people attacked the Tutsi in the country. The government suppressed the rioting. The Tutsi established control over the Burundi police and military.</p>
<p>Mwambutsa was deposed in 1966 by his son, <a href="/wiki/Ntare_V_of_Burundi" title="Ntare V of Burundi">Prince Ntare V</a>, who claimed the throne. Later that same year, Tutsi Prime Minister, then Captain Michel Micombero, deposed Ntare. Micombero abolished the monarchy and declared the nation a republic, though it was in effect a military regime.<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1972, an all-Hutu organization known as Umugambwe w'Abakozi b'Uburundi or Burundi Workers' Party (UBU) organized and carried out systematic attacks on ethnic Tutsi, with the declared intent of annihilating the whole group.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> The military regime responded with large-scale reprisals targeting Hutus. The total number of casualties was never established, but estimates for the Tutsi genocide and the <a href="/wiki/Burundi_genocide" title="Burundi genocide" class="mw-redirect">reprisals on the Hutus</a> together are said to exceed 100,000. As many refugees and asylum-seekers left the country for Tanzania and Rwanda.</p>
<p>In 1976, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Bagaza" title="Jean-Baptiste Bagaza">Jean-Baptiste Bagaza</a> (Tutsi) led a bloodless coup and promoted various reforms. His administration drafted a new constitution in 1981, which maintained Burundi as a one-party state.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> In August 1984, Bagaza was elected head of state. During his tenure, Bagaza suppressed political opponents and religious freedoms.</p>
<p>Major <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Buyoya" title="Pierre Buyoya">Pierre Buyoya</a> (Tutsi) overthrew Bagaza in 1987 and suspended the constitution, dissolving the political parties. He reinstated military rule under the Military Committee for National Salvation (CSMN).<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Anti-Tutsi ethnic propaganda disseminated by the remnants of the 1972 UBU, which had re-organized as PALIPEHUTU in 1981, led to killings of Tutsi peasants in the northern communes of Ntega and Marangara in August 1988. The death toll was put at 5,000<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> by the government, though some international NGOs believe this understates the losses.</p>
<p>The new regime did not unleash the harsh reprisals of 1972. Its effort to gain trust was eroded when it decreed an <a href="/wiki/Amnesty" title="Amnesty">amnesty</a> for those who had called for, carried out, and taken credit for the killings. Many analysts consider this period as the beginning of the "culture of impunity." But other analysts consider the "culture of impunity" to have started from 1965 and 1972, when the revolt of a small and identifiable number of Hutus unleashed massive killings of Tutsis on the whole territory.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In the aftermath of the killings, a group of Hutu intellectuals wrote an open letter to Pierre Buyoya, asking for more representation of the Hutu in the administration. The signatories were arrested and jailed. A few weeks later, Buyoya appointed a new government, with an equal number of Hutu and Tutsi among his ministers. He appointed <a href="/wiki/Adrien_Sibomana" title="Adrien Sibomana">Adrien Sibomana</a> (Hutu) as Prime Minister. Buyoya also created a commission to address issues of national unity.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1992, the government promulgated a new constitution that provided for a multi-party system.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Civil war broke out.</p>
<p>An estimated 250,000 people died in Burundi from the combined conflicts between 1962 and 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> Since Burundi's independence in 1962, there have been two events called <a href="/wiki/Burundi_genocide" title="Burundi genocide" class="mw-redirect">genocides</a> in the country. The 1972 mass killings of Hutus by the Tutsi-dominated army,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> and the 1993 mass killings of Tutsis by the Hutu majority. Both are described as genocide in the final report of the International Commission of Inquiry for Burundi presented in 2002 to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ICIBFR-85-496_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICIBFR-85-496-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_attempt_at_democracy">First attempt at democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: First attempt at democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In June 1993, <a href="/wiki/Melchior_Ndadaye" title="Melchior Ndadaye">Melchior Ndadaye</a>, leader of the Hutu-dominated <a href="/wiki/Front_for_Democracy_in_Burundi" title="Front for Democracy in Burundi">Front for Democracy in Burundi</a> (FRODEBU), won the first democratic election. He became the first Hutu head of state, leading a pro-Hutu government. In October 1993, Tutsi soldiers assassinated Ndadaye, which resulted in years of violence between Hutu and Tutsi. It is estimated that some 300,000 people, mostly civilians, were killed in the years following the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1994, the parliament elected <a href="/wiki/Cyprien_Ntaryamira" title="Cyprien Ntaryamira">Cyprien Ntaryamira</a> (Hutu) to the office of president. He and the president of Rwanda <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Habyarimana_and_Ntaryamira" title="Assassination of Habyarimana and Ntaryamira" class="mw-redirect">died together when their airplane was shot down</a>. More refugees started fleeing to Rwanda. Speaker of Parliament, <a href="/wiki/Sylvestre_Ntibantunganya" title="Sylvestre Ntibantunganya">Sylvestre Ntibantunganya</a> (Hutu), was appointed as president in October 1994. A coalition government involving 12 of the 13 parties was formed. A feared general massacre was averted, but violence broke out. A number of Hutu refugees in the capital,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Bujumbura, were killed. The mainly Tutsi Union for National Progress withdrew from the government and parliament.</p>
<p>In 1996, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Buyoya" title="Pierre Buyoya">Pierre Buyoya</a> (Tutsi) took power through a <a href="/wiki/1996_Burundian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1996 Burundian coup d'état">coup d’état</a>. He suspended the constitution and was sworn in as president in 1998. In response to rebel attacks, the government forced much of the population to relocate to refugee camps.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Under Buyoya's rule, long peace talks started, mediated by South Africa. Both parties signed agreements in <a href="/wiki/Arusha" title="Arusha">Arusha</a>, Tanzania and <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a>, South Africa, to share power in Burundi. The agreements took four years to plan.</p>
<p>On August 28, 2000, a transitional government for Burundi was planned as a part of the Arusha Peace and Reconciliation Agreement. The transitional government was placed on a trial basis for five years. After several aborted cease-fires, a 2001 peace plan and power-sharing agreement has been relatively successful. A cease-fire was signed in 2003 between the Tutsi-controlled Burundian government and the largest Hutu rebel group, CNDD-FDD (National Council for the Defense of Democracy-Forces for the Defense of Democracy).<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2003, FRODEBU leader <a href="/wiki/Domitien_Ndayizeye" title="Domitien Ndayizeye">Domitien Ndayizeye</a> (Hutu) was elected president.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> In early 2005, ethnic quotas were formed for determining positions in Burundi's government. Throughout the year, elections for parliament and president occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pierre_Nkurunziza" title="Pierre Nkurunziza">Pierre Nkurunziza</a> (Hutu), once a leader of a rebel group, was elected president. As of 2008, the Burundian government is talking with the Hutu-led Palipehutu-National Liberation Forces (NLF)<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> to bring peace to the country.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_agreements">Peace agreements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Peace agreements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>African leaders began a series of peace talks between the warring factions following a request by the United Nations Secretary General <a href="/wiki/Boutros_Boutros-Ghali" title="Boutros Boutros-Ghali">Boutros Boutros-Ghali</a> for them to intervene in the humanitarian crisis. Talks were initiated under the aegis of former Tanzanian President <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nyerere" title="Julius Nyerere">Julius Nyerere</a> in 1995; following his death, South African President <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> took the helm. As the talks progressed, South African President <a href="/wiki/Thabo_Mbeki" title="Thabo Mbeki">Thabo Mbeki</a> and United States President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> also lent their respective weight.</p>
<p>The peace talks took the form of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Track_I_mediation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Track I mediation (page does not exist)">Track I mediations</a>. This method of negotiation can be defined as a form of diplomacy involving governmental or intergovernmental representatives, who may use their positive reputations, mediation, or the “carrot and stick” method as a means of obtaining or forcing an outcome, frequently along the lines of “bargaining” or “win-lose”.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The main objective was to transform the Burundian government and military structurally in order to bridge the ethnic gap between the Tutsi and Hutu. It was to take place in two major steps. First, a transitional power-sharing government would be established, with the presidents holding office for three-year terms. The second objective involved a restructuring of the military, where the two groups would be represented equally.</p>
<p>As the protracted nature of the peace talks demonstrated, the mediators and negotiating parties confronted several obstacles. First, the Burundian officials perceived the goals as “unrealistic” and viewed the treaty as ambiguous, contradictory and confusing. Second, and perhaps most importantly, the Burundians believed the treaty would be irrelevant without an accompanying cease fire. This would require separate and direct talks with the rebel groups. The main Hutu party was skeptical of the offer of a power-sharing government; they alleged that they had been deceived by the Tutsi in past agreements.</p>
<p>In 2000, the Burundian President signed the treaty, as well as 13 of the 19 warring Hutu and Tutsi factions. Disagreements persisted over which group would preside over the nascent government and when the ceasefire would begin. The spoilers of the peace talks were the hardliner Tutsi and Hutu groups who refused to sign the accord; as a result, violence intensified. Three years later at a summit of African leaders in Tanzania, the Burundian president and the main opposition Hutu group signed an accord to end the conflict; the signatory members were granted ministerial posts within the government. However, smaller militant Hutu groups – such as the Forces for National Liberation – remained active.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="UN_involvement">UN involvement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: UN involvement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Between 1993 and 2003, many rounds of peace talks, overseen by regional leaders in <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, South Africa, and <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, gradually established power-sharing agreements to satisfy the majority of the contending groups. Initially the <a href="/w/index.php?title=South_African_Protection_Support_Detachment&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="South African Protection Support Detachment (page does not exist)">South African Protection Support Detachment</a> was deployed to protect Burundian leaders returning from exile. These forces became part of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=African_Union_Mission_to_Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="African Union Mission to Burundi (page does not exist)">African Union Mission to Burundi</a>, deployed to help oversee the installation of a transitional government. In June 2004, the UN stepped in and took over peacekeeping responsibilities as a signal of growing international support for the already markedly advanced peace process in Burundi.<sup id="cite_ref-Howard.2C_Lise_Morje_2008_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howard.2C_Lise_Morje_2008-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The mission’s mandate, under <a href="/wiki/Chapter_VII_of_the_UN_Charter" title="Chapter VII of the UN Charter" class="mw-redirect">Chapter VII of the UN Charter</a>, has been to monitor cease-fire; carry out disarmament, demobilization, and reintegration of former combatants; support humanitarian assistance and refugee and IDP return; assist with elections; protect international staff and Burundian civilians; monitor Burundi’s troublesome borders, including halting illicit arms flows; and assist in carrying out institutional reforms including those of the Constitution, judiciary, armed forces, and police. The mission has been allotted 5,650 military personnel, 120 civilian police, and about 1,000 international and local civilian personnel. The mission has been functioning well. It has greatly benefited from the transitional government, which has functioned and is in the process of transitioning to one that will be popularly elected.<sup id="cite_ref-Howard.2C_Lise_Morje_2008_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howard.2C_Lise_Morje_2008-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The main difficulty in the early stages was continued resistance to the peace process by the last Hutu nationalist rebel group. This organization continued its violent conflict on the outskirts of the capital despite the UN’s presence. By June 2005, the group had stopped fighting, and its representatives were brought back into the political process. All political parties have accepted a formula for inter-ethnic power-sharing: no political party can gain access to government offices unless it is ethnically integrated.<sup id="cite_ref-Howard.2C_Lise_Morje_2008_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howard.2C_Lise_Morje_2008-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The focus of the UN’s mission had been to enshrine the power-sharing arrangements in a popularly voted constitution, so that elections may be held and a new government installed. Disarmament, demobilization and reintegration were done in tandem with elections preparations. In February 2005, the Constitution was approved with over 90% of the popular vote. In May, June, and August 2005, three separate elections were also held at the local level for the Parliament and the presidency.</p>
<p>While there are still some difficulties with refugee returns and securing adequate food supplies for the war-weary population, the mission has managed to win the trust and confidence of a majority of the formerly warring leaders, as well as the population at large.<sup id="cite_ref-Howard.2C_Lise_Morje_2008_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howard.2C_Lise_Morje_2008-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> It has also been involved with several “quick effect” projects, including rehabilitating and building schools, orphanages, health clinics, and rebuilding infrastructure such as water lines.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2006_to_present">2006 to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2006 to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BujumburaFromCathedral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/BujumburaFromCathedral.jpg/220px-BujumburaFromCathedral.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/BujumburaFromCathedral.jpg/330px-BujumburaFromCathedral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/BujumburaFromCathedral.jpg/440px-BujumburaFromCathedral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BujumburaFromCathedral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of the capital city Bujumbura in 2006.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Reconstruction efforts in Burundi started to practically take effect after 2006. The UN shut down its peacekeeping mission and re-focused on helping with reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-accessed_on_29-10-08_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-accessed_on_29-10-08-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Toward achieving <a href="/wiki/Economic_reconstruction" title="Economic reconstruction">economic reconstruction</a>, Rwanda, D.R.Congo and Burundi relaunched the regional <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_the_Great_Lakes_Countries" title="Economic Community of the Great Lakes Countries">Economic Community of the Great Lakes Countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-accessed_on_29-10-08_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-accessed_on_29-10-08-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, Burundi, along with Rwanda, joined the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> in 2007.</p>
<p>However, the terms of the September 2006 Ceasefire between the government and the last remaining armed opposition group, the <a href="/wiki/National_Liberation_Front_(Burundi)" title="National Liberation Front (Burundi)">FLN</a> (Forces for National Liberation, also called NLF or FROLINA), were not totally implemented, and senior FLN members subsequently left the truce monitoring team, claiming that their security was threatened.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> In September 2007, rival FLN factions clashed in the capital, killing 20 fighters and causing residents to begin fleeing. Rebel raids were reported in other parts of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-accessed_on_29-10-08_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-accessed_on_29-10-08-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> The rebel factions disagreed with the government over disarmament and the release of political prisoners.<sup id="cite_ref-hrw.org_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hrw.org-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In late 2007 and early 2008, FLN combatants attacked government-protected camps where former combatants were living. The homes of rural residents were also pillaged.<sup id="cite_ref-hrw.org_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hrw.org-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The 2007 report<sup id="cite_ref-hrw.org_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hrw.org-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> mentions many areas where improvement is required. Civilians are victims of repeated acts of violence done by the FLN. The latter also recruits child soldiers. The rate of violence against women is high. Perpetrators regularly escape prosecution and punishment by the state. There is an urgent need for reform of the judicial system. <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">Genocide</a>, <a href="/wiki/War_crimes" title="War crimes" class="mw-redirect">war crimes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crimes_against_humanity" title="Crimes against humanity">crimes against humanity</a> remain unpunished. The establishment of a <a href="/wiki/Truth_and_Reconciliation_Commission" title="Truth and Reconciliation Commission" class="mw-redirect">Truth and Reconciliation Commission</a> and a Special <a href="/wiki/Tribunal" title="Tribunal">Tribunal</a> for investigation and prosecution has not yet been implemented. The freedom of expression is limited; journalists are frequently arrested for carrying out legitimate professional activities. A total of 38,087 Burundian refugees have been repatriated between January and November 2007.</p>
<p>In late March 2008, the FLN sought for the parliament to adopt a law guaranteeing them ‘provisional immunity’ from arrest. This would cover ordinary crimes, but not grave violations of international humanitarian law like war crimes or crimes against humanity .<sup id="cite_ref-hrw.org_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hrw.org-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Even though the government has granted this in the past to people, the FLN has been unable to obtain the provisional immunity.</p>
<p>On April 17, 2008, the FLN bombarded Bujumbura. The Burundian army fought back and the FLN suffered heavy losses. A new ceasefire was signed on May 26, 2008. In August 2008, President Nkurunziza met with the FLN leader <a href="/wiki/Agathon_Rwasa" title="Agathon Rwasa">Agathon Rwasa</a>, with the mediation of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Nqakula" title="Charles Nqakula">Charles Nqakula</a>, South Africa’s Minister for Safety and Security. This was the first direct meeting since June 2007. Both agree to meet twice a week to establish a commission to resolve any disputes that might arise during the peace negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Refugee camps are now closing down, and 450,000 refugees have returned. The economy of the country is shattered – as of 2011 Burundi has one of the lowest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">per capita gross incomes</a> in the world. With the return of refugees, amongst others, property conflicts have started.</p>
<p>Burundi now participates in African Union peacekeeping missions, including the mission to Somalia against Al-Shahab militants.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pierre_Nkurunziza_-_World_Economic_Forum_on_Africa_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Pierre_Nkurunziza_-_World_Economic_Forum_on_Africa_2008.jpg/220px-Pierre_Nkurunziza_-_World_Economic_Forum_on_Africa_2008.jpg" width="220" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Pierre_Nkurunziza_-_World_Economic_Forum_on_Africa_2008.jpg/330px-Pierre_Nkurunziza_-_World_Economic_Forum_on_Africa_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Pierre_Nkurunziza_-_World_Economic_Forum_on_Africa_2008.jpg/440px-Pierre_Nkurunziza_-_World_Economic_Forum_on_Africa_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="669" data-file-height="1000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pierre_Nkurunziza_-_World_Economic_Forum_on_Africa_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pierre_Nkurunziza" title="Pierre Nkurunziza">Pierre Nkurunziza</a>, President of Burundi.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Burundi" title="Politics of Burundi">Politics of Burundi</a></div>
<p>Burundi's political system is that of a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> republic based upon a multi-party state. The President of Burundi is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>. There are currently 21 <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_Burundi" title="List of political parties in Burundi">registered parties in Burundi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> On March 13, 1992, Tutsi coup leader <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Buyoya" title="Pierre Buyoya">Pierre Buyoya</a> established a constitution,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> which provided for a multi-party political process<sup id="cite_ref-PH_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PH-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> and reflected multi-party competition. Six years later, on June 6, 1998, the constitution was changed, broadening <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Burundi" title="National Assembly of Burundi" class="mw-redirect">National Assembly</a>'s seats and making provisions for two vice presidents. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Accord" title="Arusha Accord" class="mw-redirect">Arusha Accord</a>, Burundi enacted a transitional government in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-unburundi_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unburundi-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Burundi's legislative branch is a <a href="/wiki/Bicameral" title="Bicameral" class="mw-redirect">bicameral</a> assembly, consisting of the Transitional National Assembly and the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Burundi" title="Senate of Burundi" class="mw-redirect">Transitional Senate</a>. As of 2004, the Transitional National Assembly consists of 170 members, with the Front for Democracy in Burundi holding 38% of seats, and 10% of the assembly is controlled by UPRONA. Fifty-two seats are controlled by other parties. Burundi's constitution mandates representation in the Transitional National Assembly to be consistent with 60% Hutu, 40% Tutsi, and 30% female members, as well as three Batwa members.<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Members of the National Assembly are elected by popular vote and serve for five-year terms.<sup id="cite_ref-Puddington_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puddington-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Transitional Senate has fifty-one members, and three seats are reserved for former presidents. Due to stipulations in Burundi's constitution, 30% of Senate members must be female. Members of the Senate are elected by electoral colleges, which consist of members from each of Burundi's provinces and communes.<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> For each of Burundi's seventeen provinces, one Hutu and one Tutsi senator are chosen. One term for the Transitional Senate is five years.<sup id="cite_ref-Puddington_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puddington-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Together, Burundi's legislative branch elect the President to a five-year term.<sup id="cite_ref-Puddington_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puddington-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Burundi's president appoints officials to his Council of Ministers, which is also part of the executive branch.<sup id="cite_ref-unburundi_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unburundi-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> The president can also pick fourteen members of the Transitional Senate to serve on the Council of Ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Members of the Council of Ministers must be approved by two-thirds of Burundi's legislature. The president also chooses two vice-presidents.<sup id="cite_ref-Puddington_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puddington-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2010, the President of Burundi is <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Nkurunziza" title="Pierre Nkurunziza">Pierre Nkurunziza</a>. The First Vice President is Therence Sinunguruza, and the Second Vice President is Gervais Rufyikiri.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Cour Supreme</i> (Supreme Court) is Burundi's highest court. There are three Courts of Appeals directly below the Supreme Court. Tribunals of First Instance are used as judicial courts in each of Burundi's provinces as well as 123 local tribunals.<sup id="cite_ref-unburundi_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unburundi-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Provinces.2C_Communes_and_Collines">Provinces, Communes and Collines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Provinces, Communes and Collines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="floatright">

</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Burundi" title="Provinces of Burundi">Provinces of Burundi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communes_of_Burundi" title="Communes of Burundi">Communes of Burundi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Collines_of_Burundi" title="Collines of Burundi">Collines of Burundi</a></div>
<p>Burundi is divided into 17 <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Burundi" title="Provinces of Burundi">provinces</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> 117 <a href="/wiki/Communes_of_Burundi" title="Communes of Burundi">communes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> and 2,638 <a href="/wiki/Collines_of_Burundi" title="Collines of Burundi">collines</a> (hills).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Provincial governments are structured upon these boundaries. In 2000, the province encompassing Bujumbura was separated into two provinces, Bujumbura Rural and Bunjumbura Mairie.<sup id="cite_ref-xlix_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xlix-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The provinces are:</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:By-map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/By-map.png/220px-By-map.png" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/By-map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/By-map.png 2x" data-file-width="328" data-file-height="329" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:By-map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of Burundi.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Burundi" title="Geography of Burundi">Geography of Burundi</a></div>
<p>One of the smallest countries in Africa, Burundi is landlocked and has an equatorial climate. Burundi is a part of the <a href="/wiki/Albertine_Rift" title="Albertine Rift">Albertine Rift</a>, the western extension of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Rift" title="East African Rift">East African Rift</a>. The country lies on a rolling <a href="/wiki/Plateau" title="Plateau">plateau</a> in the center of Africa. The average elevation of the central plateau is 1,707&#160;m (5,600&#160;ft), with lower elevations at the borders. The highest peak, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Heha" title="Mount Heha">Mount Heha</a> at 2,685&#160;m (8,810&#160;ft),<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> lies to the southeast of the capital, Bujumbura. The source of the <a href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River" class="mw-redirect">Nile River</a> is in Bururi province, and is linked from Lake Victoria to its headwaters via the <a href="/wiki/Ruvyironza_River" title="Ruvyironza River">Ruvyironza River</a><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2010)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> is also an important water source, which serves as a fork to the <a href="/wiki/Kagera_River" title="Kagera River">Kagera River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Another major lake is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, located in much of Burundi's southwestern corner.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Burundi's lands are mostly agricultural or pasture. Settlement by rural populations has led to <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion" class="mw-redirect">soil erosion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Habitat_loss" title="Habitat loss" class="mw-redirect">habitat loss</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Deforestation of the entire country is almost completely due to <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a>, with a mere 600&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (230&#160;sq&#160;mi) remaining and an ongoing loss of about 9% per annum.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> There are two <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national parks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kibira_National_Park" title="Kibira National Park">Kibira National Park</a> to the northwest (a small region of rain forest, adjacent to <a href="/wiki/Nyungwe_Forest_National_Park" title="Nyungwe Forest National Park" class="mw-redirect">Nyungwe Forest National Park</a> in Rwanda), <a href="/wiki/Ruvubu_National_Park" title="Ruvubu National Park">Ruvubu National Park</a> to the northeast (along the <a href="/wiki/Rurubu_River" title="Rurubu River">Rurubu River</a>, also known as Ruvubu or Ruvuvu). Both were established in 1982 to conserve wildlife populations.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Burundi" title="Economy of Burundi">Economy of Burundi</a></div>
<div class="hatnote boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_companies_based_in_Burundi" title="List of companies based in Burundi">List of companies based in Burundi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tree_map_export_2009_Burundi.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tree_map_export_2009_Burundi.jpeg/220px-Tree_map_export_2009_Burundi.jpeg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tree_map_export_2009_Burundi.jpeg/330px-Tree_map_export_2009_Burundi.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tree_map_export_2009_Burundi.jpeg/440px-Tree_map_export_2009_Burundi.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1105" data-file-height="865" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tree_map_export_2009_Burundi.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graphical depiction of Burundi's product exports in 28 color-coded categories.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Burundi is one of the world's poorest countries, owing in part to its landlocked geography,<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> poor legal system, lack of economic freedom, lack of access to education, and the proliferation of HIV/AIDS. Approximately 80% of Burundi's population lives in poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Famines and food shortages have occurred throughout Burundi, most notably in the 20th century,<sup id="cite_ref-wein5_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wein5-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> and according to the <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a>, 56.8% of children under age five suffer from chronic <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> One scientific study of 178 nations rated Burundi's population as having the lowest <a href="/wiki/Satisfaction_with_Life_Index" title="Satisfaction with Life Index">satisfaction with life</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> As a result of poverty, Burundi is dependent on foreign aid.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burundi_-_Lake_Tanganyika_fisheries.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Burundi_-_Lake_Tanganyika_fisheries.jpg/220px-Burundi_-_Lake_Tanganyika_fisheries.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Burundi_-_Lake_Tanganyika_fisheries.jpg/330px-Burundi_-_Lake_Tanganyika_fisheries.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Burundi_-_Lake_Tanganyika_fisheries.jpg/440px-Burundi_-_Lake_Tanganyika_fisheries.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burundi_-_Lake_Tanganyika_fisheries.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fishermen on Lake Tanganyika.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Burundi's largest industry is agriculture, which accounted for just over 30% of the GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_agriculture" title="Subsistence agriculture">Subsistence agriculture</a> accounts for 90% of agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> The nation's largest source of revenue is coffee, which makes up 93% of Burundi's exports.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Other agricultural products include cotton, tea, maize, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, sweet potatoes, bananas, <a href="/wiki/Manioc" title="Manioc" class="mw-redirect">manioc</a> (tapioca); beef, milk, and hides. Some of Burundi's natural resources include <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, nickel, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>, copper, and <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Besides agriculture, other industries include: assembly of imported components; public works construction; food processing, and light consumer goods such as blankets, shoes, and soap. Burundi's currency is the <a href="/wiki/Burundian_franc" title="Burundian franc">Burundian franc</a> (BIF); as of May 26, 2012, 1,371.00 Burundian franc were equivalent to one United States dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In regards to telecommunications infrastructure, Burundi is ranked 2nd to last in the World Economic Forum's Network Readiness Index (NRI) – an indicator for determining the development level of a country’s information and communication technologies. Burundi ranked number 147 overall in the 2014 NRI ranking, down from 144 in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Lack of access to financial services is a serious problem for the majority of the population, particularly in the densely populated rural areas: only 2% of the total population holds bank accounts, and fewer than 0.5% use bank lending services. <a href="/wiki/Microfinance" title="Microfinance">Microfinance</a>, however, plays a larger role, with 4% of Burundians being members of microfinance institutions – a larger share of the population than that reached by banking and postal services combined. 26 licensed microfinance institutions (MFIs) offer savings, deposits, and short- to medium-term credit. Dependence of the sector on donor assistance is limited.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Burundi is part of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> and a potential member of the planned <a href="/wiki/East_African_Federation" title="East African Federation">East African Federation</a>. Economic growth in Burundi is relatively steady but Burundi has a lot of work to do to catch up to other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Karuzi_Burundi_goats.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Karuzi_Burundi_goats.jpg/220px-Karuzi_Burundi_goats.jpg" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Karuzi_Burundi_goats.jpg/330px-Karuzi_Burundi_goats.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Karuzi_Burundi_goats.jpg/440px-Karuzi_Burundi_goats.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="864" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Karuzi_Burundi_goats.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A group of Burundian women rearing goats.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Children_in_Bujumbura.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Children_in_Bujumbura.jpg/220px-Children_in_Bujumbura.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Children_in_Bujumbura.jpg/330px-Children_in_Bujumbura.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Children_in_Bujumbura.jpg/440px-Children_in_Bujumbura.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Children_in_Bujumbura.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Children in Bujumbura, Burundi</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Burundi" title="Demographics of Burundi">Demographics of Burundi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Burundi" title="Languages of Burundi">Languages of Burundi</a></div>
<p>As of July 2012, Burundi is projected to have an estimated population of 10,557,259 people. This estimate explicitly takes into account the effects of AIDS, which has a significant effect on the demographics of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Over 500,000 have been displaced owing to the disease.<sup id="cite_ref-xlix_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xlix-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many Burundians have migrated to other countries as a result of the civil war. In 2006, the United States accepted approximately 10,000 Burundian refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Burundi remains an overwhelmingly rural society, with just 13% of the population living in urban areas in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> The population density of around 315 people per square kilometer (753 per sq&#160;mi) is the second highest in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state_10-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Roughly 85% of the population are of <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a> ethnic origin, 15% are <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsi</a>, and fewer than 1% are indigenous <a href="/wiki/Twa" title="Twa">Twa</a>/Pygmies.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Burundi has the fifth highest <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rate</a> in the world, at 6.08 children born/woman (2012 estimates).<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Burundi" title="Religion in Burundi">Religion in Burundi</a></div>
