</div>
<p>Sources estimate the Christian population at 80–90%, with Roman Catholics representing the largest group at 60–65%. <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> practitioners constitute the remaining 15–25%. An estimated 5% of the population adheres to traditional indigenous religious beliefs. Muslims constitute 2–5%, the majority of whom are <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunnis</a> and live in urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pew_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-state2010_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state2010-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Burundi" title="Health in Burundi">Health in Burundi</a></div>
<p>Burundi is a landlocked, resource-poor country with an underdeveloped manufacturing sector. The economy is predominantly agricultural; agriculture accounts for just over 30% of GDP and employs more than 90% of the population. Burundi's primary exports are coffee and tea, which account for 90% of foreign exchange earnings, though exports are a relatively small share of GDP. Burundi's export earnings – and its ability to pay for imports – rests primarily on weather conditions and international coffee and tea prices. An ethnic-based war that lasted for over a decade resulted in more than 200,000 deaths, forced more than 48,000 refugees into Tanzania, and displaced 140,000 others internally. Only one in two children go to school, and approximately one in 15 adults has HIV/AIDS. Food, medicine, and electricity remain in short supply. Less than 2% of the population has electricity in its homes. Burundi's GDP grew around 4% annually in 2006–12. Political stability and the end of the civil war have improved aid flows and economic activity has increased, but underlying weaknesses – a high poverty rate, poor education rates, a weak legal system, a poor transportation network, overburdened utilities, and low administrative capacity – risk undermining planned economic reforms. The purchasing power of most Burundians has decreased as wage increases have not kept up with inflation. Burundi will remain heavily dependent on aid from bilateral and multilateral donors – foreign aid represents 42% of Burundis national income, the second highest rate in Sub-Saharan Africa. Burundi joined the East African Community in 2009, which should boost Burundi's regional trade ties, and also in 2009 received $700 million in debt relief. Government corruption is hindering the development of a healthy private sector as companies seek to navigate an environment with ever changing rules.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gitega_drums.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Gitega_drums.JPG/220px-Gitega_drums.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Gitega_drums.JPG/330px-Gitega_drums.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Gitega_drums.JPG/440px-Gitega_drums.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gitega_drums.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Drums from <a href="/wiki/Gitega" title="Gitega">Gitega</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Burundi" title="Culture of Burundi">Culture of Burundi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Burundi" title="Music of Burundi">Music of Burundi</a></div>
<p>Burundi's culture is based on local tradition and the influence of neighboring countries, though cultural prominence has been hindered by <a href="/wiki/Civil_unrest" title="Civil unrest" class="mw-redirect">civil unrest</a>. Since farming is the main industry, a typical Burundian meal consists of <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potatoes" title="Sweet potatoes" class="mw-redirect">sweet potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peas" title="Peas" class="mw-redirect">peas</a>. Due to the expense, meat is eaten only a few times per month. When several Burundians of close acquaintance meet for a gathering they drink impeke, a beer, together from a large container to symbolize unity. Notable Burundians include the soccer player <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Tchit%C3%A9" title="Mohammed Tchité" class="mw-redirect">Mohammed Tchité</a> and singer Jean Pierre Nimbona, popularly known as Kidumu (who is based in Nairobi, Kenya).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Crafts are an important art form in Burundi and are attractive gifts to many tourists. Basket weaving is a popular craft for Burundian artisans.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Other crafts such as masks, shields, statues and pottery are made in Burundi.<sup id="cite_ref-r1_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r1-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Drumming is an important part of the Burundian cultural heritage. The world-famous <a href="/wiki/Royal_Drummers_of_Burundi" title="Royal Drummers of Burundi">Royal Drummers of Burundi</a>, who have performed for over forty years, are noted for traditional drumming using the <a href="/wiki/Karyenda" title="Karyenda">karyenda</a>, amashako, ibishikiso, and ikiranya drums.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Dance often accompanies drumming performance, which is frequently seen in celebrations and family gatherings. The abatimbo, which is performed at official ceremonies and rituals, and the fast-paced abanyagasimbo are some famous Burundian dances. Some musical instruments of note are the flute, <a href="/wiki/Zither" title="Zither">zither</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ikembe" title="Ikembe">ikembe</a>, indonongo, <a href="/wiki/Umuduri" title="Umuduri">umuduri</a>, inanga, and the inyagara.<sup id="cite_ref-r1_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r1-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Le_Match.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Le_Match.jpg/220px-Le_Match.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Le_Match.jpg/330px-Le_Match.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Le_Match.jpg/440px-Le_Match.jpg 2x" data-file-width="721" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Le_Match.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Football in Burundi.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kirundi" title="Kirundi">Kirundi</a>, French, and <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a> are spoken throughout Burundi.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Burundi's <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a> is strong, relaying history and life lessons through storytelling, poetry, and song. Imigani, indirimbo, amazina, and ivyivugo are types of literary genres existing in Burundi.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Basketball and track and field are noted sports in Burundi. Martial arts are popular, as well. There are five major judo clubs: Club Judo de l'Entente Sportive, in Downtown, and four others throughout the city.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">Association football</a> is a popular pastime throughout the country, as are <a href="/wiki/Mancala" title="Mancala">mancala</a> games.</p>
<p>Most Christian holidays are celebrated in Burundi, with Christmas being the largest.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Burundian Independence Day is celebrated annually on July 1.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2005, the Burundian government declared <a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Fitr" title="Eid al-Fitr">Eid al-Fitr</a>, an Islamic holiday, to be a public holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-Burundi_celebrates_Muslim_holiday_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burundi_celebrates_Muslim_holiday-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2009, the government of Burundi changed the law to criminalise homosexuality. Persons found guilty of consensual same-sex relations risk two to three years in prison and a fine of 50,000 to 100,000 Burundian francs. Amnesty International has condemned the action, calling it a violation of Burundi’s obligations under international and regional human rights law, and against the constitution, which guarantees the right to privacy.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carolus_Magnus_Schule-Burundi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Carolus_Magnus_Schule-Burundi.jpg/220px-Carolus_Magnus_Schule-Burundi.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Carolus_Magnus_Schule-Burundi.jpg/330px-Carolus_Magnus_Schule-Burundi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Carolus_Magnus_Schule-Burundi.jpg/440px-Carolus_Magnus_Schule-Burundi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carolus_Magnus_Schule-Burundi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carolus Magnus School in Burundi. The school benefits from the campaign "Your Day for Africa" by Aktion Tagwerk.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Burundi" title="Education in Burundi">Education in Burundi</a></div>
<p>In 2009, the adult <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a> rate in Burundi was estimated to be 67% (73% male and 61% female), with a literacy rate of 77% and 76%, respectively, for men and women between the ages of 15 to 24.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Literacy among adult women in Burundi has increased by 17% since 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Burundi's literacy rate is low due to low school attendance and because literacy in Kirundi only provides access to materials printed in that language. Ten percent of Burundian boys are allowed a secondary education.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Burundi has the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Burundi" title="University of Burundi">University of Burundi</a>. There are several museums in the cities, such as the <a href="/wiki/Burundi_Geological_Museum" title="Burundi Geological Museum">Burundi Geological Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bujumbura" title="Bujumbura">Bujumbura</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Burundi_National_Museum" title="Burundi National Museum">Burundi National Museum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Burundi_Museum_of_Life" title="Burundi Museum of Life">Burundi Museum of Life</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gitega" title="Gitega">Gitega</a>.</p>
<p>There will also be a new school opening in one of the poorest regions of Burundi, Rusaga, which is funded by an English charity called the 'Burundi Education Foundation'. The Burundi Education Foundation is hoping to open the school in the summer of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010 a new elementary school was opened in the small village of Rwoga, Burundi which is funded by the students of Westwood High School, Quebec, Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Since 2015, <a href="/wiki/Hills_University" title="Hills University">Hills University</a> provides free courses that students can register for and follow in real time, such as MOOCs (Massive Open Online Courses) and courses that offer all of their materials freely online under an intellectual property license, including video lectures, notes, and assignments.</p>
